reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a causal 1D convolution: left-padded so each output only depends on current and past inputs.
    Used in autoregressive sequence models (Mamba, Hyena, WaveNet, TCN) for causal sequence processing.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of input channels.
        out_channels (int): Number of output channels.
        kernel_size (int): Size of the convolution kernel.
        dilation (int, optional): Dilation rate. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If True, adds a learnable bias. Defaults to False.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, dilation: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.causal_padding = (kernel_size - 1) * dilation
        self.conv1d = nn.Conv1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation=dilation, bias=bias)

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        x = torch.nn.functional.pad(x, (self.causal_padding, 0))
        return self.conv1d(x)

batch_size = 32
in_channels = 256
out_channels = 256
kernel_size = 4
dilation = 1
seq_len = 8192

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(batch_size, in_channels, seq_len)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation]
turn 0llm latency: 18.3s
reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel that replicates a causal 1D convolution. The reference model: - Causal 1D convolution with left padding of `(kernel_size - 1) * dilation`. - `nn.Conv1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation=dilation, bias=bias)`. - Input shape: `[batch_size, in_channels, seq_len]`. - Output shape: `[batch_size, out_channels, seq_len]` because left padding exactly cancels the rightward shift. So operation is essentially a 1D convolution over the time dimension with groups=1. We need to write a custom CUDA kernel to speed this up. We can use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` with a `c++` source containing `torch::Tensor` wrapper and a raw CUDA kernel. The required class `ModelNew` must have same constructor args. It should compute the convolution manually or use e.g. a gemm approach (im2col) or direct convolution. We have to produce numerically equivalent output at fp32. Tolerance is 1e-4. We need to optimize for speed. Let's consider hardware specs. Let's first call `get_gpu_specs` to know target GPU.

reasoning
Let me analyze the problem. The reference model performs a causal 1D convolution. It pads the input on the left with `(kernel_size - 1) * dilation` zeros, then applies a `Conv1d`. For the example: - batch_size = 32 - in_channels = 256 - out_channels = 256 - kernel_size = 4 - dilation = 1 - seq_len = 8192 This is essentially a Conv1d with left padding. The standard PyTorch implementation will likely: 1. Pad the input ( adding padding to tensor) 2. Run cuDNN/cuBLAS convolution For a small kernel and dilation=1, this is a relatively straightforward operation. We can write a custom kernel that directly computes the convolution without explicitly padding, which saves memory bandwidth. Key considerations: - Input shape: [batch_size, in_channels, seq_len] = [32, 256, 8192] - Weight shape: [out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size] = [256, 256, 4] - Bias shape (optional): [out_channels] - Output shape: [batch_size, out_channels, seq_len] = [32, 256, 8192] Since in_channels = out_channels = 256 and kernel_size = 4, this is essentially a GEMM-like operation. Each output element is dot(w[o, :, :], x[b, :, t:t+4]) (with appropriate padding for early timesteps). Given the sizes, the operation is memory-bound. The arithmetic intensity is low because kernel_size=4 means only 4 * 256 = 1024 multiplications per output channel, but reading 256 input channels for each of the 4 positions. Actually let me compute more carefully. For each output position (b, o, t): - Multiply-adds: in_channels * kernel_size = 256 * 4 = 1024 - Input reads: in_channels * kernel_size = 1024 (amortized across same b, o, t reusing inputs) - Weight reads: out_channels * in_channels * kernel_size = 256K (but cached if reused) - Output: 1 write Actually since weights are reused heavily, and the batch is 32, the main bottleneck is reading input data. One approach: For each output channel, this is a 1D conv. We could tile across sequence length and batch, and use kernel unrolling. Given the H100 has 80GB HBM3 with 3.35 TB/s bandwidth, this will definitely be memory bandwidth bound. A simple fused kernel where each thread handles one (batch, out_channel, time) position would be very simple: - Load the weight for this out_channel (256 * 4 = 1024 floats = 4KB) - For each time t, iterate over in_channels c and kernel positions k: - if t + k - padding >= 0: acc += weight[o, c, k] * x[b, c, t + k - padding] But wait, padding = (kernel_size - 1) * dilation = 3. So for output t (0-indexed), we need input at positions t-3, t-2, t-1, t. But with left padding of 3, the padded input positions are 0 to seq_len+2, and output at position t corresponds to padded input at position t+3. Actually let me re-read: `pad(x, (self.causal_padding, 0))` pads left with `causal_padding` and right with 0. So if x is [B, C, L], the padded x is [B, C, L + causal_padding], and conv1d with kernel_size=4, stride=1 will output [B, out_channels, L + causal_padding - 3] = [B, out_channels, L]. Wait, actually `nn.Conv1d` with kernel_size=4 on input of length L+causal_padding outputs length L+causal_padding - 4 + 1 = L + causal_padding - 3. causal_padding = 3, so output length = L. Yes that matches. So for output position t in [0, L-1], it reads from padded input positions [t, t+1, t+2, t+3], which correspond to original input positions [t-3, t-2, t-1, t], with negative indices being "padded" (value 0). So a direct computation for output[b, o, t] = sum_{c=0}^{in_channels-1} sum_{k=0}^{3} weight[o, c, k] * (t - 3 + k >= 0 ? x[b, c, t - 3 + k] : 0) Or we can think of it as: output[b, o, t] = sum_{c=0}^{255} [weight[o, c, 0] * (t>=3 ? x[b, c, t-3] : 0) + weight[o, c, 1] * (t>=2 ? x[b, c, t-2] : 0) + weight[o, c, 2] * (t>=1 ? x[b, c, t-1] : 0) + weight[o, c, 3] * x[b, c, t]] For a simple kernel, each thread can handle one (b, o) pair and iterate over t, or one (b, o, t) element. Given large seq_len=8192 and batch*out=8192, we have 32*256*8192 = 67M output elements. Each needs 1024 FMAs. If each thread computes one output element: - Load weight[0:256*4] for this o - but we don't want to load all that into registers. Instead we can load weight[o, c, k] as needed. - But loading weight on the fly from global memory for every output element would be terrible. - Better: have threads in a block share the same o, and load weight into shared memory? But 256 out channels, and weight for one o is 256*4 = 1024 floats = 4KB. That's manageable. But each block would need different weights. Actually a much better approach is to use CUTLASS or a GEMM formulation. Note that Conv1d with kernel_size=4 can be viewed as a matrix multiplication if we unfold the input. Specifically, for each batch element b, we can form a matrix: - Rows: out_channels - Cols: seq_len - The operation is: output[b] = W * X_unfolded[b] where W is [out_channels, in_channels * kernel_size] and X_unfolded[b] is [in_channels * kernel_size, seq_len]. But X_unfolded is formed by extracting patches. For each t and for each c and k, X_unfolded[(c, k), t] = x[b, c, t - 3 + k] (with appropriate zero padding). This is exactly what `nn.Unfold` or `im2col` does, but doing it explicitly would use a lot of memory. Instead, we can write a kernel that computes the GEMM on-the-fly. On an H100, we can potentially use Tensor Cores by treating this as a batched GEMM. However, with kernel_size=4, the K dimension would be in_channels * kernel_size = 1024, and M=out_channels=256, N=seq_len=8192, batch=32. So each GEMM is 256x8192 x 1024. That's decent for Tensor Cores (K=1024 is good). Actually, we can use `cutlass` or write a simple kernel. But writing a custom Tensor Core kernel is complex. Let me think about a more practical approach: a simple CUDA kernel that fuses the padding and convolution. This avoids the memory bandwidth of writing/reading the padded tensor. PyTorch's Conv1d will likely call cuDNN which is highly optimized, but it operates on the padded tensor. The padding itself is cheap but the extra memory movement might hurt. For a kernel_size=4 conv, a direct element-wise kernel where each thread handles one output element would do 1024 FMAs per thread. With 67M outputs, that's 67T FMAs total. At 67 TFLOPS FP32, that's 1 second - way too slow for a compute-bound reading. But wait, 67M * 1024 = 68.7B FMAs = 137B flop. At 67 TFLOPS, that's about 2ms. But memory bandwidth will dominate. Actually, let me think about traffic: - Reading input x: 32 * 256 * 8192 floats = 67.1M floats = 268 MB - Reading weights: 256 * 256 * 4 = 262K floats = 1 MB (reused heavily, mostly cached in L2/L1) - Writing output: 32 * 256 * 8192 = 268 MB - Total HBM traffic if perfectly cached weights: ~536 MB - At 3.35 TB/s: 536MB / 3.35TB/s = 0.16 ms But that's the ideal. Realistically, a naive kernel will have much worse memory traffic. PyTorch reference involves: 1. Padding: reads x (268MB), writes padded (268MB + small), total ~536MB 2. Conv1d via cuDNN: reads padded, reads weights, writes output cuDNN is very efficient and likely uses implicit GEMM or Winograd. But it still needs to read the padded input. The padding operation in PyTorch is probably fused into the conv by cuDNN if we're lucky, but the explicit `F.pad` might materialize the tensor. Our custom kernel can skip materializing the padded tensor and directly read from x with appropriate bounds checks. This saves the bandwidth of writing and reading the padded region. For the kernel itself, I think a straightforward approach is: - Treat as batched GEMV across output positions - Each block handles a chunk of (batch, out_channels, seq_len) - Use shared memory for weights - Coalesce global memory reads for x Let me sketch a kernel: ```cuda // B = batch, C_in = in_channels, C_out = out_channels, L = seq_len, K = kernel_size, P = padding // x: [B, C_in, L] // w: [C_out, C_in, K] // out: [B, C_out, L] // Each block handles (block_size_b, block_size_o, block_size_t) // Thread layout: can be 1D for simplicity __global__ void causal_conv1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int L, int K, int P ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C_out * L; if (idx >= total) return; int t = idx % L; int tmp = idx / L; int o = tmp % C_out; int b = tmp / C_out; float acc = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < C_in; c++) { for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { int x_idx = t - P + k; float val = 0.0f; if (x_idx >= 0) { val = x[b * C_in * L + c * L + x_idx]; } acc += w[o * C_in * K + c * K + k] * val; } } if (bias) acc += bias[o]; out[idx] = acc; } ``` This kernel is extremely simple but has terrible memory access patterns: - Weight access: each thread reads `C_in * K = 1024` floats along `o * C_in * K + c * K + k`. Since threads in a warp will have different `o`, weight accesses are scattered. Actually, if threads in a warp have same `o` but different `t`, then weights are the same and can be broadcast/kept in L1. But with 1D thread indexing where `idx` increments linearly, adjacent threads will have adjacent `t`, same `o` (if blockDim.x < L) or adjacent `o` (if blockDim.x > L). Actually in the layout: t is fastest varying, so adjacent threads have same b, same o, adjacent t. This is great! Threads in a warp will read the same weights (excellent for L1 cache) and adjacent x values (excellent coalescing for x). For x access: thread with index idx reads x at position [b, c, t - P + k]. Adjacent threads read x at [b, c, (t+1) - P + k] = [b, c, t - P + k + 1]. So adjacent threads read adjacent x elements - perfect coalescing! For weights: all threads in a warp read the same weight[o, c, k] because o is the same across the warp. This means weight reads are broadcast from L1 cache after the first thread brings them in. Excellent! So this naive kernel actually has fantastic memory coalescing for x and fantastic weight reuse across the warp. The main issue is: - Register usage: we need loops over c (256) and k (4), which is fine. - But 1024 iterations with 1 FMA each - that's 1024 FMAs per thread. At instruction throughput, an H100 can do many FMAs per cycle. - Memory: each thread reads 1024 floats from x. But x reads are coalesced across the warp, and each warp reads 32 floats per load. For K=4, we have 4 passes over x (one per k), each reading 256*32 floats? No, for each k and c, we read 1 float per thread. Wait, no - for each c, for each k, each thread reads one x float. So total x reads per thread = C_in * K = 1024 floats = 4KB. At 32 threads/warp, that's 128KB per warp. With L1 cache, since adjacent warps will read overlapping or adjacent x regions, this might be okay. Actually, a much better approach: swap loops. For each k (kernel position), all threads in a warp read a contiguous chunk of x for all c - but c varies in the outer loop. So for fixed k and c, each thread reads x[b, c, t - P + k]. For c+1, they read x[b, c+1, t - P + k], which is L floats away in memory. This strided access across channels is bad for L1 cache because channels are stored contiguously in the innermost dimension? Wait. PyTorch default layout for Conv1d input is [B, C, L], so x[b, c, t] = x[b * C * L + c * L + t]. So x[b, c+1, t] is L elements away. For L=8192, that's a big jump. Adjacent warps might have different b or o, so they might not share L1 cache effectively. Hmm, actually in our kernel, for fixed b, o, we iterate over c. The x accesses for different c are very far apart. This means for each k, we read x[b, 0, t-P+k], x[b, 1, t-P+k], etc., each separated by L=8192 floats = 32KB. On H100, L1 cache line is 128 bytes = 32 floats. So each x access loads 32 adjacent floats. But we only need 1 float per thread (or 32 floats per warp for 32 threads with adjacent t). For c=0, warp reads x[b, 0, t0-P+k : t0-P+k+31]. For c=1, reads x[b, 1, t0-P+k : t0-P+k+31]. These are 8192 floats apart. The L1 cache on H100 is 256KB per SM? Actually H100 L1 is shared with texture cache and is configurable up to ~256KB (I need to check). Regardless, 32*8192*4 bytes = 1MB, which won't fit in L1. So each x read for a new c will likely miss L1 and have to go to L2 or HBM. Wait, but the total x size is 268MB. L2 cache on H100 is 50MB. This won't fit either. So x reads will hit HBM. The key insight: each output element needs K * C_in = 1024 x reads. Total x reads = 67M * 1024 = 68.7B floats = 275GB. That's a lot of HBM traffic. In contrast, the theoretical minimum is reading each x element ~K times (actually, each x element at position t is read for outputs at t, t+1, t+2, t+3 because of the 4-wide kernel, so ~4 times on average). Minimum x traffic = 4 * 268MB = 1.07GB. Plus output 268MB, total ~1.34GB. So our naive kernel reads 275GB from HBM instead of 1.34GB - a 200x blowup! That's because weights are also read from HBM for every output element, but weights should fit in L2. Actually, wait. PyTorch Conv1d via cuDNN doesn't have this blowup. cuDNN uses tiling and shared memory to keep weights in registers/shared memory and reuses input elements across output positions. The naive element-wise kernel is terrible. We need a tiled approach. A much better kernel design: - Each block handles a tile of output positions: e.g., multiple output channels and a chunk of sequence length. - Load weights for those output channels into shared memory or keep them in registers. - Process a tile of input channels at a time, loading the corresponding x patch into shared memory. - Compute partial sums. This is basically a Conv1d implementation, or we can view it as a matrix multiplication. For simplicity and performance, let me consider using a CUTLASS-based GEMM or writing a kernel that does im2col on the fly in shared memory. Actually, let me reconsider: with kernel_size=4, the operation is very similar to a strided GEMM. Let me try a different approach: For each batch element, we want out[o, t] = sum_c sum_k W[o, c, k] * X[c, t+k-P]. This can be rewritten as: For each k in 0..3: For each c in 0..255: W_k[o, c] * X_k[c, t] Where W_k is [out_channels, in_channels] and X_k is x shifted by (k-P). The output is sum_k W_k @ X_k. For each k, W_k @ X_k is a GEMM of size [256, 256] x [256, L], resulting in [256, L]. We can accumulate across k. So total: 4 batched GEMMs. Each GEMM is 256x8192 x 256x8192? No, W_k is [256, 256], X_k is [256, L]. So it's M=256, K=256, N=L=8192. This is a classic GEMM! We can call cuBLAS 4 times and accumulate. But the X_k matrices are just shifted views of x, and we need zero-padding for the first few elements. Wait, for k=0: x shifted by -3 (t-3). For t=0,1,2 this is zero. For k=1: shift by -2. Etc. For k=3: shift by 0 (x itself). So: - k=0: W[:, :, 0] @ x[:, :-3] with padding on left (3 zeros) - k=1: W[:, :, 1] @ x[:, :-2] with padding on left (2 zeros) - k=2: W[:, :, 2] @ x[:, :-1] with padding on left (1 zero) - k=3: W[:, :, 3] @ x[:, :] (no shift) We can write this as: out = zeros out[:, :, 3:] += W[:, :, 0] @ x ( GEMM, then assign with offset) out[:, :, 2:] += W[:, :, 1] @ x out[:, :, 1:] += W[:, :, 2] @ x out[:, :, 0:] += W[:, :, 3] @ x But doing 4 separate GEMMs introduces a lot of overhead. Each GEMM is 256 x 8192 with K=256. M=256, N=8192, K=256 for each GEMM. FLOPs = 2 * M * N * K = 2 * 256 * 8192 * 256 = 1.07 GFLOPs per GEMM. 4 GEMMs = 4.3 GFLOPs total. But we have batch=32, so total = 138 GFLOPs. Wait, earlier I said 137B FLOPs. Let me recheck: 32 * 256 * 8192 * 256 * 4 * 2 = 32 * 256 * 8192 * 1024 * 2 = 137,438,953,472 = 137 GFLOPs. Yes. Each GEMM: 137/4 = 34.4 GFLOPs. At 67 TFLOPS, each GEMM takes ~0.5ms, total ~2ms. But cuBLAS has overhead, and doing 4 calls with accumulation and offset might not be great. Another approach: we can reshape W to [256, 1024] and use a custom im2col to transform x to [1024, 8192]. But im2col explicitly materializes the unfolded matrix, which is [1024, 8192] = 8M floats = 32MB per batch = 1GB for batch=32. That's a lot of memory. A fused kernel is better. Let me think about a more sophisticated CUDA kernel with shared memory tiling. Kernel design: - Grid: one block per (batch_block, out_channel_block, seq_block) - Block: maybe 256 threads - Each block computes a tile of outputs: e.g., 8 output channels x 32 sequence positions? - Or maybe 1 output channel x blockDim.x sequence positions? Actually, for Conv1d with kernel_size=4, a natural tiling approach: - Each block processes a contiguous chunk of sequence (e.g., 256 time steps) for a set of output channels (e.g., 4 or 8). - Load weights for these output channels into shared memory (4 o-chans * 256 c-in * 4 k = 4096 floats = 16KB). - Process input channels in tiles. For each tile of in_channels (e.g., 32 or 64), load the corresponding x segment into shared memory. - Since x is accessed for windows of size 4, for a tile of t in [t_start, t_start+T), we need x in [t_start-3, t_start+T+3). Load this halo region. Wait, for dilation=1, causal padding=(4-1)*1=3. Let's formalize: output at time t depends on x at times [t-3, t-2, t-1, t]. So for a block processing t in [t_start, t_end), it needs x in [t_start-3, t_end-1]. If we process T time steps per block (e.g., 128), we need x window of size T+3. But x also varies across in_channels. For a tile of C_in (e.g., 32 input channels), we need x[b, c_tile:c_tile+32, t_start-3:t_end-1]. So shared memory per block: - Weights: O_TILE * C_TILE * K floats - Input: C_TILE * (T + K - 1) floats For O_TILE=4, C_TILE=32, K=4, T=128: - Weights: 4 * 32 * 4 = 512 floats = 2KB - Input: 32 * 131 = 4192 floats = 16.8KB - Total: ~19KB - very manageable! Each thread in the block would handle some (o, t) pairs within the tile. With 256 threads, we have 4*128=512 output elements. Each thread handles 2 elements. Actually, let me think about performance. With this tiling: - We load each x element into shared memory once per block, and it's reused across all output channels in the block and across all threads processing different t. But wait, each x at [c, t_x] is used for outputs at t = t_x+3, t_x+2, t_x+1, t_x (for the 4 kernel positions). So within the block, x is reused for 4 output time positions and O_TILE output channels. Hmm actually the reuse is limited because K=4 is small. But the main benefit is coalesced global memory access for x and weights, and better data reuse for weights. A simpler but still very effective approach: let me try just using a kernel where threads compute one output channel at a time but coalesce reads across the sequence. Or, even better, I can use the fact that on H100, I might be able to write a kernel that uses vectorized loads and achieves high bandwidth. Actually, wait. I should check if PyTorch's reference is already very fast. Let me first think about whether the problem is actually memory-bound or compute-bound. Reference implementation: 1. F.pad(x, (3, 0)): This is a memory-bound copy of 268MB -> 268MB (with padding extension). Actually it just creates a new tensor and copies. 2. conv1d: cuDNN can fuse padding if it sees the pattern, but here we're explicitly padding first, so it might not fuse. Even if cuDNN gets the padded tensor, it will use highly optimized implicit GEMM. For M=256, K=1024, N=8192, this is actually a decent GEMM size. M=256, N=8192, K=1024 (since it's Conv1d, the effective K is in_channels * kernel_size = 1024). FLOPs = 2 * 256 * 8192 * 1024 = 4.3 GFLOPs per batch element? Wait no: 2 * M * N * K = 2 * 256 * 8192 * 1024 = 4,294,967,296 = 4.3 GFLOPs per batch item. For batch=32: 137 GFLOPs. Memory bandwidth: reading weights (1MB) + reading padded input (268MB + padding) + writing output (268MB) = ~537MB plus some overhead. At 3.35 TB/s, minimum time is ~0.16ms for memory. Compute minimum at 67 TFLOPS is ~2ms. So it's compute-bound! Wait, 137 GFLOPs / 67 TFLOPS = 2ms. But cuDNN achieves much higher utilization on GEMMs. For a GEMM of this size, cuDNN might achieve 50%+ utilization, so ~1ms or less. Actually wait, I realize the K for cuDNN in implicit GEMM is in_channels * kernel_size = 1024. And the weight matrix is [out_channels, K] = [256, 1024]. What if PyTorch's Conv1d is actually calling a GEMM-based implementation (via cuDNN implicit GEMM) but the kernel_size=4 and small M=256 might result in lower efficiency than a direct convolution kernel? For a direct convolution kernel, each thread computes one output element and does a dot product. Since the kernel is only 4 taps, we can unroll it. And we only need 256 * 4 = 1024 multiply-adds per output. The overhead of GEMM packing might dominate. A direct kernel could be faster for small kernels! In fact, for small kernels (K=3, 4, 5), direct convolution can be faster than GEMM-based approaches because it avoids the overhead of im2col/unrolling. Let me design a fast direct convolution kernel: ```cuda __global__ void causal_conv1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int L, int K ) { // K is kernel_size, padding = K - 1 int P = K - 1; // Each thread handles one output element (b, o, t) int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C_out * L; if (idx >= total) return; int t = idx % L; int tmp = idx / L; int o = tmp % C_out; int b = tmp / C_out; float acc = bias ? bias[o] : 0.0f; int x_base = b * C_in * L + t - P; int w_base = o * C_in * K; // Unroll K=4 for (int c = 0; c < C_in; c++) { int x_c_base = x_base + c * L; int w_c_base = w_base + c * K; float x0 = (t >= P - 0) ? input[x_c_base + 0] : 0.0f; float x1 = (t >= P - 1) ? input[x_c_base + 1] : 0.0f; float x2 = (t >= P - 2) ? input[x_c_base + 2] : 0.0f; float x3 = (t >= P - 3) ? input[x_c_base + 3] : 0.0f; acc += weight[w_c_base + 0] * x0; acc += weight[w_c_base + 1] * x1; acc += weight[w_c_base + 2] * x2; acc += weight[w_c_base + 3] * x3; } output[idx] = acc; } ``` Wait, the memory access pattern here: for adjacent threads (same b, same o, adjacent t): - x_c_base + 0, x_c_base + 1, etc. Adjacent threads read x_c_base + thread_t + offset. Since thread_t are consecutive, reads are coalesced. - weight[w_c_base + k] are identical across the warp (same o, c, k). Broadcast from L1. - Stride between c and c+1 is L floats. This is large (32KB) and might hurt L1 cache. But actually, H100 L1 cache is large enough (around 256KB) that for a warp reading 32 floats across c, and the next c being 32KB away, we won't get hits. Each read goes to L2. But L2 is 50MB, which can hold some of the input. Total input is 268MB, so L2 hit rate will be partial. Actually, the bigger issue is that each thread reads C_in * K = 1024 floats. For a warp of 32 threads: 32768 floats = 128KB per c,k combination. Actually per c iteration, each warp reads 32 floats (one per thread for each k), so 128 floats per c. Over 256 c: 32768 floats = 128KB per warp. But at t stride 1, adjacent warps will read adjacent memory regions, so warps running on the same SM might share L2 cache. Wait, I should think about this differently. If the block processes a range of t values for one (b, o), then all threads in the block read the same weights. We should definitely put weights in shared memory or at least make sure they're in L1. Better design: - Process multiple output channels per block, or multiple batch elements. - Use a 2D thread block where one dimension is t and another is o? - Or 1D thread block with block processing multiple outputs. Actually, let me think about a tiled kernel more carefully. Tiled Kernel Design: - Thread block: 128 threads - Each block computes 8 output channels x 1024 time steps? No, too much. Better: - Thread block: 256 threads (2 warps) - Organized as 32 threads for time dimension x 8 threads for output channel dimension. - Each block computes a tile of 8 output channels x some time steps, say 32. - Time tile T = 32, O_TILE = 8. - Total outputs per block: 8 * 32 = 256. Each thread handles 1 output. - For each input channel c (0 to 255): - Load weights[8 output channels, c, 0:4] into shared memory: 8 * 4 = 32 floats. - Load input x[b, c, t_start-3 : t_start+32] into shared memory: 35 floats. For all 8? No, for the one c: 35 floats. - Wait, with T=32 time steps, we need x from t_start-3 to t_start+31-1 = t_start+30? No, output at t uses x at t, t-1, t-2, t-3 (with padding). For t in [t_start, t_start+T), x needed is [t_start-3, t_start+T-1]. Size = T + 3 = 35. - For C_TILE input channels processed together: C_TILE * 35 floats. This is small enough that we could process C_in one channel at a time. Let me refine: - BlockDim = (32, 8) -> 256 threads: t_idx (0..31) and o_idx (0..7). - Grid: For each (b, o_block, t_block). - O_TILE = 8, T_TILE = 32. - Shared memory: - weight_smem[8][4] = 32 floats = 128 bytes. - x_smem[35] = 35 floats = 140 bytes. - Total per channel iteration: 268 bytes. But we process 256 channels sequentially, reusing smem. Wait, but x_smem only holds 35 floats for ONE input channel. That's tiny. We could process C_TILE channels together: - C_TILE = 32. - x_smem[32][35] = 1120 floats = 4.5KB. - weight_smem[8][32][4] = 1024 floats = 4KB. - Total: ~8.5KB - very reasonable! Then: - Outer loop over c in steps of 32 - Each iteration: - Cooperatively load weight_smem[8][32][4] - Cooperatively load x_smem[32][35] for the appropriate times and channels - Synchronize - Each thread computes partial sum over c_tile (32) and all k (4) With blockDim (32, 8), thread (tid, oid) computes output at time t_block*32 + tid, channel o_block*8 + oid. For c_tile loop: load weights for o=0..7, c=c_base..c_base+31, k=0..3 into smem load x for c=c_base..c_base+31, t=t_block*32-3..t_block*32+31-1 into smem __syncthreads() for c=0..31: for k=0..3: if (t_block*32 + tid - 3 + k >= 0): acc += weight_smem[oid][c][k] * x_smem[c][tid - 3 + k + 3] (tid - 3 + k + 3 = tid + k) else: acc += 0 Let me think about the indexing more carefully. For output time t (which is t_block*T + threadIdx.x, where T=32), we need x at [c, t + k - P] for k in [0, K-1], P=K-1=3. So x indices: t-3, t-2, t-1, t. Within the tile, t ranges from [t_block*T, t_block*T + T - 1]. So x needed: [t_block*T - 3, t_block*T + T - 1]. In smem, we store x for these indices relative to t_block*T: [-3, T-1]. So valid smem indices: 0 to T+2 (size T+3=35). For a given output time t_tile = threadIdx.x (0..31), x_smem index for kernel k is: t_tile + k - P + P = t_tile + k? Wait, if smem stores starting at t_block*T - 3, then: - x at global t_block*T - 3 goes to smem[0] - x at global t goes to smem[t - (t_block*T - 3)] = smem[t - t_block*T + 3] For t = t_block*T + t_tile, smem index = t_tile + 3. For kernel position k, we need x at t + k - 3, smem index = t_tile + k. So: `x_smem[c_local][t_tile + k]` where c_local is 0..31. This requires `t_tile + k < T + 3 = 35`, which is always true since t_tile <= 31 and k <= 3, so t_tile + k <= 34. For boundary condition: when t_block == 0 and t_tile < 3, for k=0 we need x at t-3 < 0, so we should pad with 0. We can handle this by setting x_smem for negative indices to 0, or checking in the loop. When loading x_smem, for global index < 0, load 0. For global index >= L, load 0 (though L is exact for output). So the algorithm is: ```cuda #define T_TILE 32 #define O_TILE 8 #define C_TILE 32 const int P = K - 1; __shared__ float weight_smem[O_TILE][C_TILE][K]; __shared__ float x_smem[C_TILE][T_TILE + K]; int b = blockIdx.z; int o_block = blockIdx.y; int t_block = blockIdx.x; int t = t_block * T_TILE + threadIdx.x; int o = o_block * O_TILE + threadIdx.y; // Initialize accumulator float acc = (bias && o < C_out) ? bias[o] : 0.0f; // Main loop over input channels for (int c_base = 0; c_base < C_in; c_base += C_TILE) { // Load weights cooperatively // Total weight elements: O_TILE * C_TILE * K = 8 * 32 * 4 = 1024 // Block has 256 threads for (int i = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; i < O_TILE * C_TILE * K; i += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int wk = i % K; int wc = (i / K) % C_TILE; int wo = i / (K * C_TILE); int global_o = o_block * O_TILE + wo; int global_c = c_base + wc; if (global_o < C_out && global_c < C_in) { weight_smem[wo][wc][wk] = weight[global_o * C_in * K + global_c * K + wk]; } else { weight_smem[wo][wc][wk] = 0.0f; } } // Load x cooperatively // x_smem[C_TILE][T_TILE + K] // Total elements: C_TILE * (T_TILE + K) = 32 * 35 = 1120 for (int i = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; i < C_TILE * (T_TILE + K); i += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int sx = i % (T_TILE + K); int sc = i / (T_TILE + K); int global_t = t_block * T_TILE + sx - P; int global_c = c_base + sc; if (global_c < C_in && global_t >= 0 && global_t < L) { x_smem[sc][sx] = input[b * C_in * L + global_c * L + global_t]; } else { x_smem[sc][sx] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); if (t < L && o < C_out) { int t_local = threadIdx.x; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_TILE && c_base + c < C_in; c++) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { acc += weight_smem[threadIdx.y][c][k] * x_smem[c][t_local + k]; } } } __syncthreads(); } if (t < L && o < C_out) { output[b * C_out * L + o * L + t] = acc; } ``` This looks good! Let me verify the arithmetic: - Each thread does C_TILE*K = 32*4 = 128 FMAs per channel block iteration. - Number of iterations: C_in / C_TILE = 256 / 32 = 8. - Total FMAs per thread: 8 * 128 = 1024. Correct for one output element. - Memory access is coalesced for both weight and x loads. - Weights are reused across T_TILE=32 time positions and O_TILE=8 output channels via shared memory. - x is loaded once and reused across O_TILE=8 output channels. Block occupancy: smem = 1024*4 bytes + 1120*4 bytes = 4096 + 4480 = 8576 bytes = ~8.5KB. Registers: each thread needs ~16-24 registers. With 256 threads/block, that's ~4096-6144 registers. Limit is 64K per SM on H100. So we can have up to 3-4 blocks per SM concurrently, giving good occupancy. Wait, actually H100 allows up to 32 blocks per SM. With 256 threads per block, we can have 2 blocks per SM (512 threads, but H100 has 2048 threads/SM, so up to 8 blocks of 256 threads). So occupancy isn't an issue. The main advantage over cuDNN/GEMM: 1. No explicit padding tensor. 2. Direct convolution with good shared memory usage. 3. K=4 is small enough that the unrolled loop is efficient. Actually, I realize we should also consider batch dimension. Currently batch is in blockIdx.z, so each block handles one batch element. Let me think about another approach: instead of my custom tiled kernel, what if I just do the GEMM formulation using `torch.matmul`? ```python # W: [out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size] # x: [batch, in_channels, seq_len] # out = sum_k W[:, :, k] @ x_shifted[:, :, :] # For k=0: needs x shifted left by 3 (padding with 0) # For k=1: shifted left by 2 # ... ``` We can do: ```python out = torch.zeros(batch, out_channels, seq_len, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) for k in range(kernel_size): shift = kernel_size - 1 - k if shift > 0: out[:, :, shift:] += weight[:, :, k] @ x[:, :, :-shift] else: out[:, :, :] += weight[:, :, k] @ x ``` Using `torch.matmul` (cuBLAS) 4 times. Let me write the kernel with the tiling approach. I'll use template parameters for flexibility but instantiate for common cases. One important thing: I should use `__launch_bounds__` to help the compiler optimize register usage. Here is my plan for the kernel: ```cuda template<int K> __global__ void causal_conv1d_kernel_templated( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int L ) { constexpr int T_TILE = 32; constexpr int O_TILE = 8; constexpr int C_TILE = 32; const int P = K - 1; __shared__ float weight_smem[O_TILE][C_TILE][K]; __shared__ float x_smem[C_TILE][T_TILE + K]; int b = blockIdx.z; int o_block = blockIdx.y; int t_block = blockIdx.x; int t = t_block * T_TILE + threadIdx.x; int o = o_block * O_TILE + threadIdx.y; float acc = 0.0f; if (bias && o < C_out && t < L) { acc = bias[o]; } for (int c_base = 0; c_base < C_in; c_base += C_TILE) { // Cooperative load of weights int n_weights = O_TILE * C_TILE * K; for (int idx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; idx < n_weights; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int wk = idx % K; int wc = (idx / K) % C_TILE; int wo = idx / (K * C_TILE); int global_o = o_block * O_TILE + wo; int global_c = c_base + wc; float val = 0.0f; if (global_o < C_out && global_c < C_in) { val = weight[(global_o * C_in + global_c) * K + wk]; } weight_smem[wo][wc][wk] = val; } // Cooperative load of x int n_x = C_TILE * (T_TILE + K); for (int idx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; idx < n_x; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int sx = idx % (T_TILE + K); int sc = idx / (T_TILE + K); int global_t = t_block * T_TILE + sx - P; int global_c = c_base + sc; float val = 0.0f; if (global_c < C_in && global_t >= 0 && global_t < L) { val = input[(b * C_in + global_c) * L + global_t]; } x_smem[sc][sx] = val; } __syncthreads(); if (t < L && o < C_out) { #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_TILE; c++) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { acc += weight_smem[threadIdx.y][c][k] * x_smem[c][threadIdx.x + k]; } } } __syncthreads(); } if (t < L && o < C_out) { output[(b * C_out + o) * L + t] = acc; } } ``` Wait, there's a bug. The `#pragma unroll` loops over C_TILE=32, but if c_base + c >= C_in, we're loading 0 but still computing. That's fine because we load 0 into both weight and x for out-of-bounds. Actually, for x we set to 0 for out-of-bounds c, and weight to 0. So acc += 0 * 0 = 0. Good. But `(threadIdx.x + k)` can exceed T_TILE+K-1? threadIdx.x is 0..31, k is 0..3. Max is 34. T_TILE+K = 35. So indices 0..34 are valid. Good. Register pressure: With the unrolled loops, the compiler might use many registers. But K is templated so for K=4 it will fully unroll. Actually, I realize `blockDim.x * blockDim.y` might not be known at compile time if I pass blockDim dynamically. But in the kernel, `blockDim.x` and `blockDim.y` are built-in variables. The loop `idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y` can't be unrolled, which is fine. Wait, there's a problem: for `if (bias && o < C_out && t < L) { acc = bias[o]; }`, but then inside the `c_base` loop, we only accumulate if `t < L && o < C_out`. Should we initialize acc with bias only if we're going to write? I'll pass bias as a pointer and check `bias != nullptr`. Actually, CUDA doesn't like `__restrict__` on pointers that might be null? No, `__restrict__` just means no aliasing, it's fine if null. I should make the kernel robust: ```cuda float acc = 0.0f; if (t < L && o < C_out && bias != nullptr) { acc = bias[o]; } ``` Then always accumulate in the loop (but only if t < L && o < C_out). Now, for the `load_inline` binding. I need CUDA source and a host wrapper function. Host wrapper: ```cuda torch::Tensor causal_conv1d_cuda(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int kernel_size) { int B = input.size(0); int C_in = input.size(1); int L = input.size(2); int C_out = weight.size(0); auto output = torch::zeros({B, C_out, L}, input.options()); dim3 block(T_TILE, O_TILE); dim3 grid((L + T_TILE - 1) / T_TILE, (C_out + O_TILE - 1) / O_TILE, B); // Dispatch based on kernel_size switch (kernel_size) { case 3: causal_conv1d_kernel_templated<3><<<grid, block>>>(...); break; case 4: causal_conv1d_kernel_templated<4><<<grid, block>>>(...); break; // ... } } ``` But wait, the kernel_size affects P = K - 1, which affects the x_smem size [C_TILE][T_TILE + K]. If K is a template parameter, this is compiled into different kernels. I should handle common K values. However, CUDA's `__shared__` memory with template parameters works fine. Wait, I need to be careful about the `block` dimensions. If I set `dim3 block(32, 8)`, then `threadIdx.x` goes 0..31 and `threadIdx.y` goes 0..7. My kernel assumes `threadIdx.x` maps to time and `threadIdx.y` maps to output channel. So `blockDim.x = T_TILE = 32` and `blockDim.y = O_TILE = 8`. Can we have cases where L < 32 or C_out < 8? Yes. The kernel handles it with boundary checks (`t < L`, `o < C_out`). One subtlety: with `t < L`, threads where `threadIdx.x >= L` don't participate in the FMA computation but they still participate in shared memory loads and the `__syncthreads()`. That's fine. Wait, if L is small (e.g., L=1), `grid.x = 1`, `block.x = 32`, but only `threadIdx.x = 0` has `t < L`. The other 31 threads still run the shared memory loading code (which they should), skip the FMA loop, and hit `__syncthreads()`. This works correctly. What about grid and block dimensions if L is not a multiple of T_TILE? The last block will have threads with `t >= L`, and they won't write to output. Now, I need to think about PyTorch Tensor memory layout. PyTorch default is NCHW for images, and for 1D it's [B, C, L]. The elements are contiguous in L, then C, then B. So `input[b][c][t]` is at `input[(b * C_in + c) * L + t]` if contiguous. I should check `.is_contiguous()` in the wrapper, or call `.contiguous()` before passing. Now let me think if there are further optimizations: 1. **Vectorized loads**: For weight loading, since K=4, we can load float4. For x loading, T_TILE+K = 35 or 36, which is not ideal. But we can pad x_smem to a multiple of 4 (e.g., 36) and load float4 for the first 32 elements, then scalar for the rest. Actually C_TILE=32 and we load sequentially, so each thread can load a float4 if indexing is right. Actually, for x_smem[C_TILE][T_TILE + K], the layout is row-major (C++ default for multidimensional arrays). So x_smem[0][0..T+K-1] are contiguous. For sc=0, sx=0..3, float4 can load 4 contiguous floats. For weight_smem[O_TILE][C_TILE][K], weight_smem[wo][wc][0..K-1] is contiguous. So for K=4, a float4 load works. Actually, I bet cuDNN's GEMM-based conv will be very hard to beat for the main conv. But maybe fusing the padding saves enough to win. Let me also consider if there's a way to do this with implicit GEMM in shared memory, essentially implementing the GEMM ourselves with the unrolling done on-the-fly. For implicit GEMM, we'd view this as: - M = C_out = 256 - N = seq_len = 8192 - K = C_in * kernel_size = 1024 And we process tiles of M, N, K in shared memory. Actually, the kernel I described IS essentially a direct convolution. Since K (the kernel height in 1D) is small, direct conv avoids the overhead of GEMM packing. For very small kernels like K=4, direct convolution can be faster than implicit GEMM because: 1. No need for workspace/memory for im2col 2. Better data reuse since the kernel is applied directly 3. The small K means the inner loop is tiny and fully unrollable I think my tiled kernel has a good chance. Wait, let me reconsider one aspect: `causal_padding = (kernel_size - 1) * dilation`. Since `dilation` is also a constructor arg, the kernel should handle arbitrary dilation. But the test uses dilation=1. For simplicity and performance, I'll write the kernel for dilation=1 but if the constructor passes a different dilation... well, I should respect it. Actually, for arbitrary dilation d, the receptive field size is (kernel_size - 1) * d + 1, and padding is (kernel_size - 1) * d. The kernel accesses x at t - padding + k * d for k in [0, K-1]. So causal_padding = P = (K - 1) * d. Access pattern: x[t - P + k * d]. For d > 1, the x_smem needs to account for the stride. The effective window size is (K - 1) * d + 1. For K=4, d=2, window = 7. So x_smem size = T_TILE + (K-1)*d + 1 = T_TILE + P + 1-ish. Wait, let me think. For output time t, accesses are at t - P + k*d for k=0..K-1. So the range is [t - P, t - P + (K-1)*d] = [t - P, t] since P = (K-1)*d. Wait, t - P + (K-1)*d = t - (K-1)*d + (K-1)*d = t. Yes. So the window is from t-P to t, which has size P+1 = (K-1)*d + 1. For d=1, P=K-1, size = K. For d=2, K=4, P=6, size = 7. In shared memory, for t in [t_block*T, t_block*T + T_TILE - 1], x needed is [t_block*T - P, t_block*T + T_TILE - 1]. Size = T_TILE + P. And x_smem index for output t with local offset t_local and kernel k is: t_local + k*d. Max = T_TILE - 1 + (K-1)*d = T_TILE - 1 + P. Since P = (K-1)*d, size needed = T_TILE + P. Actually, for d > 1, some positions in x_smem are unused (e.g., for d=2, K=4, we use positions 0, 2, 4, 6 relative to the start). We still need to store them all to have correct indexing, or we can remap. To support arbitrary dilation, I'll make P a runtime parameter and adjust indexing: ```cuda if (t < L && o < C_out) { #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_TILE; c++) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { int x_idx = threadIdx.x + k * dilation; if (x_idx < T_TILE + P) { acc += weight_smem[threadIdx.y][c][k] * x_smem[c][x_idx]; } } } } ``` Wait, `threadIdx.x + k * dilation` can exceed `T_TILE + P - 1`? - Max t_local = T_TILE - 1 - Max k = K - 1 - Max x_idx = T_TILE - 1 + (K-1) * d = T_TILE - 1 + P - x_smem size = T_TILE + P - Index T_TILE - 1 + P is within bounds since max index is T_TILE + P - 1. Good. For dilation=2, K=4: x_idx values are t_local, t_local+2, t_local+4, t_local+6. x_smem columns 0 to T_TILE+6-1. We load x values for sx in [0, T_TILE+P-1]. Actually, wait. With runtime dilation, `x_smem[c][sx]` corresponds to global_t = t_block*T_TILE + sx - P. So `x_smem[c][t_local + k*d]` corresponds to global_t = t_block*T_TILE + t_local + k*d - P = t + k*d - P. That's exactly what we want. So the kernel can handle arbitrary dilation with `P = (K - 1) * dilation`. What about K? If K is not known at compile time, we can't fully unroll the inner loop. But we can template K. Let me write the kernel with templated K and runtime P (which depends on K and dilation). Actually, I can compute P at runtime from K and dilation. But the x_smem size depends on P... wait, P = (K-1) * dilation. If K is a template parameter, then (K-1)*dilation is not known at compile time if dilation is runtime. Hmm, C++ requires array sizes to be compile-time constants (except for C++ dynamic arrays, but CUDA shared memory prefers static sizes). For dynamic P, I could declare: ```cuda extern __shared__ float shared_mem[]; ``` and partition it into weight_smem and x_smem. But the indexing becomes more complex. Since dilation is small (typically 1 or small values), I could either: 1. Use runtime P with dynamic shared memory. 2. Pad x_smem to a maximum expected size. 3. Make both K and dilation template parameters. Actually, since the benchmark uses dilation=1 and K=4, and typical use cases have small K and dilation, I can just use a flexible approach. Option: dynamic shared memory. ```cuda extern __shared__ float shared_mem[]; // In the kernel: float* weight_smem = shared_mem; float* x_smem = shared_mem + O_TILE * C_TILE * K; // Access: weight_smem[(wo * C_TILE + wc) * K + wk] // Access: x_smem[sc * (T_TILE + P) + sx] ``` This allows any P at runtime. The indexing is slightly more expensive but shared memory is fast. Let me use dynamic shared memory for flexibility. But I need to pass the shared memory size to the kernel launch: ```cuda size_t smem_size = O_TILE * C_TILE * K * sizeof(float) + C_TILE * (T_TILE + P) * sizeof(float); kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(...); ``` Wait, if K is templated, O_TILE*C_TILE*K is known at compile time. But P depends on dilation (runtime). So total smem = compile_time_size + runtime_size. We can compute it. Actually, for the benchmark config (K=4, d=1, P=3), x_smem size = C_TILE * (T_TILE + 3) = 32 * 35 = 1120 floats. weight_smem = 8 * 32 * 4 = 1024 floats. Total = 2144 floats = 8576 bytes. Perfect. Now, one more consideration: if the dilation is large, P becomes large and x_smem grows. For the benchmark it'll be small. Let me write the actual CUDA code. I'll use template parameter for K to allow unrolling, and dynamic shared memory split into two arrays. Actually, you know what? Let me try a different, perhaps even simpler approach first. PyTorch/cuDNN might fuse the padding into the conv. This could be much faster than the explicit pad + conv. But is it faster enough? And more importantly, does it count as "custom CUDA kernel"? Wait, I just realized: for C_in=256 and K=4, the inner product per output is 1024 FMAs. A naive kernel with good memory access might actually be quite competitive with cuDNN for this specific small-kernel case, especially since we can avoid the padding tensor entirely. One more optimization idea: process multiple batch elements per block, or use the batch dimension more effectively. Currently z-dispatch means each SM gets blocks from different batches. Since the batches are independent and x doesn't fit in L2, batching in z doesn't help with cache reuse. But it does provide enough parallelism. Actually, H100 has 50MB L2 cache. For our problem: - x size = 32 * 256 * 8192 * 4 = 268MB - weight size = 256 * 256 * 4 * 4 = 1MB - output size = 268MB Since x and output are much larger than L2, there's no reuse across batch or output channels for x. The only reuse is for weights. So our kernel's primary job is to: 1. Keep weights in fast memory (L1 or shared) as long as possible. 2. Coalesce all global memory accesses. Our tiled kernel does both. Let me also consider: what if I use float4 for accumulation and math? No, float32 accumulation is fine. The kernel should still work (P=0, x_smem size = T_TILE). And what about bias? The reference uses `bias=bias` in the Conv1d constructor. If bias=True, the conv adds bias. OK, here's my code plan: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template <int K> __global__ void causal_conv1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int L, int dilation ) { const int T_TILE = 32; const int O_TILE = 8; const int C_TILE = 32; const int P = (K - 1) * dilation; extern __shared__ float shared_mem[]; float* weight_smem = shared_mem; // [O_TILE][C_TILE][K] float* x_smem = shared_mem + O_TILE * C_TILE * K; // [C_TILE][T_TILE + P] int b = blockIdx.z; int o_block = blockIdx.y; int t_block = blockIdx.x; int t = t_block * T_TILE + threadIdx.x; int o = o_block * O_TILE + threadIdx.y; float acc = (bias != nullptr && t < L && o < C_out) ? bias[o] : 0.0f; int block_stride = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; int thread_id = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; for (int c_base = 0; c_base < C_in; c_base += C_TILE) { // Load weights int n_weights = O_TILE * C_TILE * K; for (int idx = thread_id; idx < n_weights; idx += block_stride) { int wk = idx % K; int wc = (idx / K) % C_TILE; int wo = idx / (K * C_TILE); int global_o = o_block * O_TILE + wo; int global_c = c_base + wc; float val = 0.0f; if (global_o < C_out && global_c < C_in) { val = weight[(global_o * C_in + global_c) * K + wk]; } weight_smem[wo * C_TILE * K + wc * K + wk] = val; } // Load x int x_cols = T_TILE + P; int n_x = C_TILE * x_cols; for (int idx = thread_id; idx < n_x; idx += block_stride) { int sx = idx % x_cols; int sc = idx / x_cols; int global_t = t_block * T_TILE + sx - P; int global_c = c_base + sc; float val = 0.0f; if (global_c < C_in && global_t >= 0 && global_t < L) { val = input[(b * C_in + global_c) * L + global_t]; } x_smem[sc * x_cols + sx] = val; } __syncthreads(); if (t < L && o < C_out) { #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_TILE; ++c) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int x_idx = threadIdx.x + k * dilation; acc += weight_smem[threadIdx.y * C_TILE * K + c * K + k] * x_smem[c * x_cols + x_idx]; } } } __syncthreads(); } if (t < L && o < C_out) { output[(b * C_out + o) * L + t] = acc; } } torch::Tensor causal_conv1d_cuda(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int kernel_size, int dilation) { int B = input.size(0); int C_in = input.size(1); int L = input.size(2); int C_out = weight.size(0); auto output = torch::empty({B, C_out, L}, input.options()); const int T_TILE = 32; const int O_TILE = 8; dim3 block(T_TILE, O_TILE); dim3 grid((L + T_TILE - 1) / T_TILE, (C_out + O_TILE - 1) / O_TILE, B); int P = (kernel_size - 1) * dilation; size_t smem_size = 0; const float* bias_ptr = nullptr; if (bias.has_value() && bias.value().defined()) { bias_ptr = bias.value().data_ptr<float>(); } const float* input_ptr = input.data_ptr<float>(); const float* weight_ptr = weight.data_ptr<float>(); float* output_ptr = output.data_ptr<float>(); switch (kernel_size) { case 2: { const int K = 2; smem_size = O_TILE * C_TILE * K * sizeof(float) + C_TILE * (T_TILE + P) * sizeof(float); causal_conv1d_kernel<K><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( input_ptr, weight_ptr, bias_ptr, output_ptr, B, C_in, C_out, L, dilation); break; } case 3: { const int K = 3; smem_size = O_TILE * C_TILE * K * sizeof(float) + C_TILE * (T_TILE + P) * sizeof(float); causal_conv1d_kernel<K><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( input_ptr, weight_ptr, bias_ptr, output_ptr, B, C_in, C_out, L, dilation); break; } case 4: { const int K = 4; smem_size = O_TILE * C_TILE * K * sizeof(float) + C_TILE * (T_TILE + P) * sizeof(float); causal_conv1d_kernel<K><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( input_ptr, weight_ptr, bias_ptr, output_ptr, B, C_in, C_out, L, dilation); break; } case 5: { const int K = 5; smem_size = O_TILE * C_TILE * K * sizeof(float) + C_TILE * (T_TILE + P) * sizeof(float); causal_conv1d_kernel<K><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( input_ptr, weight_ptr, bias_ptr, output_ptr, B, C_in, C_out, L, dilation); break; } default: { // Fallback: generic kernel? For now just launch with K as template max? // We should handle generic case with a dynamically looped K. TORCH_CHECK(false, "Unsupported kernel_size: ", kernel_size); } } return output; } ''' module = load_inline( name='causal_conv1d', cpp_sources='', cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['causal_conv1d_cuda'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'], ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation=1, bias=False): super().__init__() self.in_channels = in_channels self.out_channels = out_channels self.kernel_size = kernel_size self.dilation = dilation self.bias = bias self.weight = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size)) if bias: self.bias_param = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(out_channels)) else: self.register_parameter('bias_param', None) # Initialize nn.init.kaiming_uniform_(self.weight, a=math.sqrt(5)) if self.bias_param is not None: fan_in, _ = nn.init._calculate_fan_in_and_fan_out(self.weight) bound = 1 / math.sqrt(fan_in) nn.init.uniform_(self.bias_param, -bound, bound) self.causal_padding = (kernel_size - 1) * dilation def forward(self, x): bias = self.bias_param if self.bias else None return module.causal_conv1d_cuda(x, self.weight, bias, self.kernel_size, self.dilation) ``` Wait, there are several issues: 1. `C_TILE` is used in the kernel but not defined in the host code. I declared `const int C_TILE = 32;` inside the kernel. The switch statement uses `C_TILE` for smem calculation. I need to make it consistent. I'll use `#define C_TILE 32` to share it. 2. `math` module import needed. 3. `c10::optional<torch::Tensor>` - I need to include the right headers. In `load_inline`, the `cuda_sources` should have `#include <torch/extension.h>` which includes everything. 4. The `load_inline` call might need `extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3']` and maybe architecture flags. 5. Wait, for `block.stride` indexing, `blockDim.x * blockDim.y` might overflow? No. 6. `weight_smem` indexing: `wo * C_TILE * K + wc * K + wk`. Since `weight_smem` is declared as `float*`, we use 1D indexing. 7. `x_smem` indexing: `sc * x_cols + sx`. 8. One issue: `blockDim.x * blockDim.y` can be larger than `n_weights` or `n_x`, which is fine because the `for` loop with `idx += block_stride` handles it. 9. Launch bounds: I should add `__launch_bounds__(T_TILE * O_TILE)` to help the compiler. Let me also think about occupancy. With 256 threads per block and smem_size around 8KB, on H100 we should get good occupancy. One potential issue: `causal_conv1d_kernel` does `#pragma unroll` for `c` and `k`. With C_TILE=32 and K=4, the inner loops are fully unrolled. This is 128 iterations. The compiler should handle this. But the register usage might be high. Let me check if I should use `#pragma unroll` for c and k. Actually, I should be careful. If C_TILE=32 and the compiler unrolls all 32 iterations, plus K=4 unrolled, that's 128 statements with loads from shared memory. The compiler might generate a lot of instructions but shared memory latency is low. For register usage: we have `acc` (1 register), loop indices (a few), shared memory addresses (a few). With unrolled loops, the compiler doesn't need loop variables for c and k at runtime (it unrolls to straight-line code). So register pressure might actually be lower. Wait, but the compiler might try to prefetch shared memory loads. For 128 FMAs, with 255 max registers per thread, we have plenty of room. Another thing: for the first few blocks where t_block=0, much of x_smem is zero (the padding). We still load zeros into x_smem. That's fine. Now, one important subtlety about `F.pad(x, (padding, 0))`: PyTorch's Conv1d with `padding=0` on the explicit pad expects the output to be exactly `L` (same as input). In our kernel, output length is `L`. For `t` from 0 to L-1, we correctly handle the causal masking via the x_smem zero-padding for negative indices. I want to double check: in the reference, `pad(x, (3, 0))` on [B, C, L] gives [B, C, L+3]. Conv1d with kernel_size=4 on [B, C, L+3] gives [B, out_channels, L+3-4+1] = [B, out_channels, L]. Correct. For our kernel, output at time 0: reads x[-3], x[-2], x[-1], x[0]. But x[-3..-1] are 0. So output[0] = w[3] * x[0] (if we map k=3 to current position). Wait, let me check indexing carefully. In the standard Conv1d definition: output[t] = sum_k weight[k] * input[t + k] (for valid conv without offset?). Actually, `nn.Conv1d` computes: output[t] = sum_{c, k} weight[o, c, k] * input_padded[c, t + k] where t ranges from 0 to output_len - 1, and input_padded is the padded input. If we pad left by 3, then input_padded[c, s] for s in [0, L+2] is: - s=0,1,2: 0 - s>=3: input[c, s-3] So output[t] = sum_{c,k} weight[o, c, k] * input_padded[c, t + k] For t=0: input_padded[c, k] for k=0,1,2,3. - k=0: input_padded[c, 0] = 0 - k=1: input_padded[c, 1] = 0 - k=2: input_padded[c, 2] = 0 - k=3: input_padded[c, 3] = input[c, 0] Wait, so output[0] = weight[o, c, 3] * input[c, 0]? That seems odd. Usually causal conv would have output[0] use only weight[..., -1] or something. Actually, let me check PyTorch Conv1d documentation. The formula is: out[i] = sum_k weight[k] * input[stride * i + k - padding] For Conv1d with kernel_size=4, dilation=1, padding=3 (left): out[i] = sum_k weight[k] * input[i + k - 3] For i=0: sum_k weight[k] * input[k - 3] - k=0: input[-3] = 0 (padded) - k=1: input[-2] = 0 - k=2: input[-1] = 0 - k=3: input[0] So yes, output[0] = weight[3] * input[0]. And for i=1: - k=0: input[-2] = 0 - k=1: input[-1] = 0 - k=2: input[0] - k=3: input[1] output[1] = weight[2]*input[0] + weight[3]*input[1] For i=3: - k=0: input[0] - k=1: input[1] - k=2: input[2] - k=3: input[3] output[3] = weight[0]*input[0] + weight[1]*input[1] + weight[2]*input[2] + weight[3]*input[3] So the causal behavior is that output[i] only depends on input[0..i]. Now, in my kernel: output at t uses x at indices: t - P + k*d for k=0..K-1. With P=(K-1)*d = 3, d=1: - k=0: t-3 - k=1: t-2 - k=2: t-1 - k=3: t And I multiply by weight[c, k]. So: output[t] = sum_c sum_k weight[c, k] * x[c, t-3+k] For t=0: k=0 uses x[-3]=0, k=1 uses x[-2]=0, k=2 uses x[-1]=0, k=3 uses x[0]. output[0] = weight[3] * x[0]. This matches PyTorch! Good. For t=3: uses x[0], x[1], x[2], x[3]. output[3] = weight[0]*x[0] + weight[1]*x[1] + weight[2]*x[2] + weight[3]*x[3]. But in PyTorch's Conv1d, out[i] = sum_k weight[k] * input[i + k - padding], which for i=3 is sum_k weight[k] * input[k]. Since input_padded[k] = input[k-3] for k>=3, wait... Actually, PyTorch conv1d formula: out(N_i, C_{out_j}) = bias(C_{out_j}) + sum_k^{C_{in}-1} weight(C_{out_j}, k) * input(N_i, k) Where * is cross-correlation: For each output position, cross-correlation means we slide the kernel and at each position, the kernel is aligned with the input. The kernel indices are not flipped (PyTorch uses cross-correlation, not convolution). The formula used by PyTorch for 1D cross-correlation with padding p is: out[i] = sum_{c, k} weight[o, c, k] * input_padded[c, i * stride + k] Wait, no. For stride=1, dilation=1: out[i] = sum_{c, k} weight[o, c, k] * input_padded[c, i + k] Actually, in `nn.Conv1d`, the kernel is applied as cross-correlation, which means no flip. For each output position, `kernel[k]` multiplies `input[i + k]`. With padding on the left, `input_padded[i + k]` for i=0, k=0 is `input_padded[0]`, which is the leftmost padded element. If left padding is 3, `input_padded[0]`, `input_padded[1]`, `input_padded[2]` are 0, and `input_padded[3]` is `input[0]`. So for out[0]: - k=0: weight[0] * 0 - k=1: weight[1] * 0 - k=2: weight[2] * 0 - k=3: weight[3] * input[0] For out[1]: - k=0: weight[0] * 0 - k=1: weight[1] * 0 - k=2: weight[2] * input[0] - k=3: weight[3] * input[1] This is exactly what my kernel computes! Because for output t=0, my kernel computes: `weight[c, 0] * x_shifted_for_k0 + weight[c, 1] * x_shifted_for_k1 + ...` where x_shifted_for_k = x[c, t - 3 + k]. Wait, but in the standard conv formula, the input index is `i + k`, while in my kernel it's `t - P + k = t - 3 + k`. For t=0: - k=0: t-3+0 = -3 -> padded 0 - k=1: -2 -> padded 0 - k=2: -1 -> padded 0 - k=3: 0 -> x[0] But note: in the PyTorch formula, `out[i]` uses `input_padded[i + k]`. For i=0, k=0: input_padded[0]; k=1: input_padded[1]; k=3: input_padded[3]. However, `input_padded[3]` corresponds to `x[0]`. So `k=3` in PyTorch corresponds to `k=3` in my formula for `t=0`, and both multiply `weight[k]`. Wait, but does PyTorch use `input_padded[i + k]` or `input_padded[i + k - padding]`? Let me think. For 1D conv with padding=3 (both sides), output at i uses input_padded at [i, i+1, i+2, i+3]. Here `i + k` for k=0..3. With left padding=3, right padding=0, the input_padded is 3 longer on the left. The input_padded index `j` corresponds to original input `j - 3`. So `input_padded[i + k] = input[i + k - 3]`. So `out[i] = sum_k weight[k] * input[i + k - 3]`. For i=0: `sum_k weight[k] * input[k - 3]`. My kernel: `out[t] = sum_k weight[k] * x[t - 3 + k]`. Yes, they match exactly. So the indexing is correct. One more thing: the output layout. In my kernel: `output[(b * C_out + o) * L + t]` For PyTorch Conv1d, output is [B, C_out, L], contiguous. So `output[b][o][t] = output[(b * C_out + o) * L + t]`. Correct. Input layout: `input[(b * C_in + c) * L + t]`. Correct for [B, C_in, L]. Weight layout: `weight[(o * C_in + c) * K + k]`. PyTorch Conv1d weight is [C_out, C_in / groups, K]. For groups=1, it's [C_out, C_in, K]. So `weight[o][c][k] = weight[(o * C_in + c) * K + k]`. Correct. Great. Now, I should test this. Let me write a first version and compile it. Let me draft the full Python module: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import math from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #define T_TILE 32 #define O_TILE 8 #define C_TILE 32 template <int K> __global__ __launch_bounds__(T_TILE * O_TILE) void causal_conv1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int L, int dilation ) { const int P = (K - 1) * dilation; const int x_cols = T_TILE + P; extern __shared__ float shared_mem[]; float* weight_smem = shared_mem; // O_TILE * C_TILE * K float* x_smem = shared_mem + O_TILE * C_TILE * K; // C_TILE * x_cols int b = blockIdx.z; int o_block = blockIdx.y; int t_block = blockIdx.x; int t = t_block * T_TILE + threadIdx.x; int o = o_block * O_TILE + threadIdx.y; float acc = 0.0f; if (bias != nullptr && t < L && o < C_out) { acc = bias[o]; } int block_stride = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; int thread_id = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; for (int c_base = 0; c_base < C_in; c_base += C_TILE) { // Load weights int n_weights = O_TILE * C_TILE * K; for (int idx = thread_id; idx < n_weights; idx += block_stride) { int wk = idx % K; int wc = (idx / K) % C_TILE; int wo = idx / (K * C_TILE); int global_o = o_block * O_TILE + wo; int global_c = c_base + wc; float val = 0.0f; if (global_o < C_out && global_c < C_in) { val = weight[(global_o * C_in + global_c) * K + wk]; } weight_smem[wo * C_TILE * K + wc * K + wk] = val; } // Load x int n_x = C_TILE * x_cols; for (int idx = thread_id; idx < n_x; idx += block_stride) { int sx = idx % x_cols; int sc = idx / x_cols; int global_t = t_block * T_TILE + sx - P; int global_c = c_base + sc; float val = 0.0f; if (global_c < C_in && global_t >= 0 && global_t < L) { val = input[(b * C_in + global_c) * L + global_t]; } x_smem[sc * x_cols + sx] = val; } __syncthreads(); if (t < L && o < C_out) { #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_TILE; ++c) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int x_idx = threadIdx.x + k * dilation; acc += weight_smem[threadIdx.y * C_TILE * K + c * K + k] * x_smem[c * x_cols + x_idx]; } } } __syncthreads(); } if (t < L && o < C_out) { output[(b * C_out + o) * L + t] = acc; } } torch::Tensor causal_conv1d_cuda(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int kernel_size, int dilation) { int B = input.size(0); int C_in = input.size(1); int L = input.size(2); int C_out = weight.size(0); auto output = torch::empty({B, C_out, L}, input.options()); dim3 block(T_TILE, O_TILE); dim3 grid((L + T_TILE - 1) / T_TILE, (C_out + O_TILE - 1) / O_TILE, B); int P = (kernel_size - 1) * dilation; size_t smem_size = 0; const float* bias_ptr = nullptr; if (bias.has_value() && bias.value().defined()) { bias_ptr = bias.value().data_ptr<float>(); } const float* input_ptr = input.data_ptr<float>(); const float* weight_ptr = weight.data_ptr<float>(); float* output_ptr = output.data_ptr<float>(); switch (kernel_size) { case 1: { smem_size = O_TILE * C_TILE * 1 * sizeof(float) + C_TILE * (T_TILE + P) * sizeof(float); causal_conv1d_kernel<1><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( input_ptr, weight_ptr, bias_ptr, output_ptr, B, C_in, C_out, L, dilation); break; } case 2: { smem_size = O_TILE * C_TILE * 2 * sizeof(float) + C_TILE * (T_TILE + P) * sizeof(float); causal_conv1d_kernel<2><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( input_ptr, weight_ptr, bias_ptr, output_ptr, B, C_in, C_out, L, dilation); break; } case 3: { smem_size = O_TILE * C_TILE * 3 * sizeof(float) + C_TILE * (T_TILE + P) * sizeof(float); causal_conv1d_kernel<3><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( input_ptr, weight_ptr, bias_ptr, output_ptr, B, C_in, C_out, L, dilation); break; } case 4: { smem_size = O_TILE * C_TILE * 4 * sizeof(float) + C_TILE * (T_TILE + P) * sizeof(float); causal_conv1d_kernel<4><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( input_ptr, weight_ptr, bias_ptr, output_ptr, B, C_in, C_out, L, dilation); break; } case 5: { smem_size = O_TILE * C_TILE * 5 * sizeof(float) + C_TILE * (T_TILE + P) * sizeof(float); causal_conv1d_kernel<5><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( input_ptr, weight_ptr, bias_ptr, output_ptr, B, C_in, C_out, L, dilation); break; } case 6: { smem_size = O_TILE * C_TILE * 6 * sizeof(float) + C_TILE * (T_TILE + P) * sizeof(float); causal_conv1d_kernel<6><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( input_ptr, weight_ptr, bias_ptr, output_ptr, B, C_in, C_out, L, dilation); break; } case 7: { smem_size = O_TILE * C_TILE * 7 * sizeof(float) + C_TILE * (T_TILE + P) * sizeof(float); causal_conv1d_kernel<7><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( input_ptr, weight_ptr, bias_ptr, output_ptr, B, C_in, C_out, L, dilation); break; } default: { // For unsupported kernel sizes, fall back to PyTorch implementation int64_t pad_left = (kernel_size - 1) * dilation; auto padded = torch::nn::functional::pad(input, torch::nn::functional::PadFuncOptions({pad_left, 0})); output = torch::conv1d(padded, weight, bias, 1, 0, dilation, 1); break; } } return output; } ''' module = load_inline( name='causal_conv1d', cpp_sources='', cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE, functions=['causal_conv1d_cuda'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'], verbose=True, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation=1, bias=False): super().__init__() self.in_channels = in_channels self.out_channels = out_channels self.kernel_size = kernel_size self.dilation = dilation self.has_bias = bias self.weight = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size)) if bias: self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(out_channels)) else: self.register_parameter('bias', None) self._reset_parameters() def _reset_parameters(self): nn.init.kaiming_uniform_(self.weight, a=math.sqrt(5)) if self.has_bias: fan_in, _ = nn.init._calculate_fan_in_and_fan_out(self.weight) bound = 1 / math.sqrt(fan_in) nn.init.uniform_(self.bias, -bound, bound) def forward(self, x): bias_opt = self.bias if self.has_bias else c10::optional<torch::Tensor>(torch::Tensor()) # Wait, we can't use c10::optional in Python! ... ``` Oops! PyTorch Conv1d formula for stride=1, dilation=d, padding=P (built-in): `out[n, c_out, l] = sum_{c_in} sum_{k=0}^{K-1} weight[c_out, c_in, k] * input[n, c_in, l + k*d - P]` If we manually pad left by P and then use built-in padding=0: - The padded input at position `j` is: `input[n, c_in, j-P]` if `j >= P`, else 0 - Conv1d with padding=0 computes: `out[n, c_out, l] = sum_{c_in} sum_k weight[c_out, c_in, k] * padded_input[n, c_in, l + k*d]` Wait, I'm second-guessing whether the dilation applies to `k` in the PyTorch Conv1d formula. Let me look this up: PyTorch Conv1d formula: `out(N_i, C_{out_j}) = bias(C_{out_j}) + sum_{k=0}^{C_{in}-1} weight(C_{out_j}, k) * input(N_i, k)` More precisely: `out[N, C_out, L] = bias[C_out] + sum_{C_in} sum_{k=0}^{K-1} weight[C_out, C_in, k] * input[N, C_in, stride * L + dilation * k - padding]` So yes, `dilation * k`. With stride=1, manual padding on the left by P=$((K-1)*dilation$), and built-in padding=0: `out[N, C_out, l] = bias + sum_{c} sum_{k} w[c_out, c, k] * padded_input[N, c, l + d*k]` Since padded_input has shape (..., L+P), and padded_input[j] = input[j-P] for j >= P, else 0. So: `out[l] = sum_c sum_k w[c_out, c, k] * input[l + d*k - P]` (with 0 if `l + d*k < P`) And since P = (K-1)*d: `l + d*k - P = l + d*k - (K-1)*d = l + d*(k - K + 1) = l - d*(K-1-k)` For k=0: `l - d*(K-1)` For k=K-1: `l` So it's causal! Let me verify with a tiny example: Given `weight` of shape `(C_out, C_in, K)`, is `weight[0, 0, 0]` multiplied with the EARLIEST or LATEST input? In PyTorch Conv1d with padding=0: `out[i] = sum_k weight[k] * input[i + k]` So `weight[0]` multiplies `input[i]` (the current/first input in the window), and `weight[K-1]` multiplies `input[i+K-1]` (the later input). With left padding P=K-1: `padded_input` has P zeros on the left. `out[i] = sum_k weight[k] * padded_input[i + k]` - `weight[0]` multiplies `padded_input[i]` = `input[i-P]` or 0 - `weight[K-1]` multiplies `padded_input[i+K-1]` = `input[i]` or `input[i+K-1-P]` Wait, for d=1: `padded_input[i+K-1] = input[i+K-1-P] = input[i]` (since P=K-1). So weight[K-1] multiplies input[i]. That means: - weight[0] → oldest input (leftmost) - weight[K-1] → newest input (rightmost) This is what I had before. In my kernel: - k=0: input[t-P] (oldest) - k=K-1: input[t] (newest) So we both have weight[k] multiplying the same temporal position. This is correct. But wait... what if PyTorch uses a DIFFERENT convention for the sliding window? In some libraries, the kernel is flipped for correlation vs convolution. PyTorch's Conv1d is actually cross-correlation, not mathematical convolution. In mathematical convolution, the kernel is flipped: `out[i] = sum_k weight[k] * input[i - k]`. In cross-correlation (what PyTorch does): `out[i] = sum_k weight[k] * input[i + k]`. Both use the same weight ordering, just with different signs in the indexing. PyTorch uses cross-correlation without flipping. This is consistent with my analysis. Actually wait, I want to triple check with a concrete example. callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "\nimport torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\ncpp_source = '''\ntorch::Tensor causal_conv1d_cuda(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int kernel_size, int dilation);\n'''\n\ncuda_source = r'''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n#include <cuda.h>\n#include <cuda_runtime.h>\n\n__global__ void causal_conv1d_direct_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ input,\n const float* __restrict__ weight,\n const float* __restrict__ bias,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n int B, int C_in, int C_out, int L, int K, int dilation\n) {\n int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n int total_threads = B * C_out * L;\n if (idx >= total_threads) return;\n \n int l = idx % L;\n int tmp = idx / L;\n int c_out = tmp % C_out;\n int b = tmp / C_out;\n \n const int P = (K - 1) * dilation;\n \n float acc = 0.0f;\n if (bias != nullptr) {\n acc = bias[c_out];\n }\n \n for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) {\n for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) {\n int t = l - P + k * dilation;\n float x_val = 0.0f;\n if (t >= 0 && t < L) {\n x_val = input[((b * C_in) + c_in) * L + t];\n }\n acc += weight[((c_out * C_in) + c_in) * K + k] * x_val;\n }\n }\n \n output[((b * C_out) + c_out) * L + l] = acc;\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor causal_conv1d_cuda(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int kernel_size, int dilation) {\n int B = input.size(0);\n int C_in = input.size(1);\n int L = input.size(2);\n int C_out = weight.size(0);\n \n auto output = torch::empty({B, C_out, L}, input.options());\n \n int total = B * C_out * L;\n const int threads = 256;\n const int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads;\n \n const float* bias_ptr = nullptr;\n if (bias.has_value() && bias.value().defined()) {\n bias_ptr = bias.value().data_ptr<float>();\n }\n \n causal_conv1d_direct_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias_ptr, output.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, C_in, C_out, L, kernel_size, dilation\n );\n \n return output;\n}\n'''\n\nmodule = load_inline(\n name='causal_conv1d_direct',\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=['causal_conv1d_cuda'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'],\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation=1, bias=False):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.causal_padding = (kernel_size - 1) * dilation\n self.conv1d = nn.Conv1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation=dilation, bias=bias)\n self.kernel_size = kernel_size\n self.dilation = dilation\n\n def forward(self, x):\n if self.kernel_size > 7 or self.kernel_size < 1:\n x = torch.nn.functional.pad(x, (self.causal_padding, 0))\n return torch.nn.functional.conv1d(x, self.conv1d.weight, self.conv1d.bias, dilation=self.dilation)\n return module.causal_conv1d_cuda(x.contiguous(), self.conv1d.weight.contiguous(), self.conv1d.bias, self.kernel_size, self.dilation)\n" }

One approach: replace our custom kernel with a call to `torch.matmul` that uses TF32 automatically. How to compute conv1d via matmul with TF32? We need to construct a matrix from input such that multiplication with weight yields conv output. As mentioned, `unfold` + `matmul` does this. But maybe we can use a faster approach: since K=4 and dilation=1, and the sequence length is large (8192), we can group the sequence dimension and perform GEMM on groups. More concretely: For each batch b and output o and time t: out[b,o,t] = sum_{c,k} w[o,c,k] * x_pad[b,c,t-P+k] where P=3. Let's define a reshaped weight W' of shape [C_out, C_in*K]: W'[o, c*K + k] = w[o, c, k]. And for each t, define input vector x_t of shape [C_in*K]: x_t[c*K + k] = x_pad[b, c, t-P+k]. Then out[b,o,t] is just dot product W'[o,:] with x_t. So for fixed b, this is matrix multiplication: out[b] = W' * X_b, where X_b is [C_in*K, L], each column is x_t. So `out[b] = W' @ X_b`. We can form `X_b` by strided views of `x_pad` rather than `unfold`. For K=4, C_in=256, L=8192, X_b[c*K + k, t] = x_pad[b, c, t-P+k]. This can be obtained as: ``` x_pad = F.pad(x, (3, 0)) # x_pad shape: [B, C_in, L+3] # We want to reshape to [B, C_in*K, L] by taking sliding windows along L. ``` One cheap way: use `as_strided` to create a view. ``` x_strided = x_pad.as_strided((B, C_in, L, K), (x_pad.stride(0), x_pad.stride(1), x_pad.stride(2), x_pad.stride(2))) ``` Wait, `x_pad` has shape [B, C, L+P]. We want `x_strided` shape [B, C, L, K] where x_strided[b, c, t, k] = x_pad[b, c, t+k]. Since dilation=1 and P=K-1, exactly. Stride for k dimension is item stride along last dim = 1. Stride for t is also 1? Actually, the base pointer for x_strided[b,c,t,k] is &x_pad[b,c,t+k]. So memory layout is not contiguous for fixed t across k? It is contiguous: x_pad[b,c,t], x_pad[b,c,t+1], ..., x_pad[b,c,t+K-1]. So for each (b,c,t), the K elements are contiguous. But across t, they overlap heavily. If we reshape x_strided to [B, C*K, L] by flattening C and K dimensions, the memory layout is not standard row-major for the [C*K, L] matrix. Let's examine memory order. For fixed b, x_pad[b,c,:] is laid out contiguously. x_strided[b,c,t,k] is at offset = b*C*(L+P) + c*(L+P) + t + k. If we flatten C and K into one dimension D=C*K, index d = c*K + k. Element at [d, t] is at offset = b*C*(L+P) + c*(L+P) + t + k = b*C*(L+P) + d*(L+P)/K?? Not linear in d because c* = floor(d/K), k = d%K. offset = b*C*(L+P) + floor(d/K)*(L+P) + t + (d%K). This is NOT equivalent to a matrix where rows are contiguous. Each row jumps by (L+P) after K elements. So we cannot use `torch.mm` directly without a copy or custom indexing. Using `as_strided` + `reshape` yields a tensor that is not contiguous and strided weirdly; `matmul` on it would be inefficient because the inner dimension is not contiguous. But `torch.matmul` can handle strided tensors; cuBLAS will handle it if the leading dimension is set appropriately. For matrix A [M, K] and B [K, N], `B` must have a proper leading dimension. Since our `X_b` has shape [C*K, L] but the actual memory strides are weird (`stride(0)` may not be 1), we can still pass it to `torch.matmul` and cuBLAS will call with appropriate `lda`, `ldb`. But performance may degrade because it is not standard layout; reading rows of X_b would lead to strided access. However, there's a trick: for K=4, C=256, the total "in_features" for GEMM is C*K=1024. We can stack C channels and K offsets as different columns? Let's think. We want `out[b] = W' @ X_b` with W' [C_out, C*K] and X_b [C*K, L]. Standard GEMM. If X_b is in column-major order, cuBLAS can handle it. Can we create X_b as a contiguous tensor efficiently? Using `as_strided` and then `.contiguous()` would require copying B*C*K*L = 1GB, taking ~0.3ms at 3TB/s. That's significant overhead but maybe still acceptable? Then GEMM is straightforward. Alternatively, we can use `torch.bmm` by forming X as [B, C*K, L] and W' as [1, C_out, C*K], then broadcasting. The GEMM time on H100 for [1024, 8192] @ [256, 1024]^T? Plus copy overhead ~100 microseconds, total ~240 microseconds. The reference might be around ~200 microseconds? Maybe not enough speedup. c_in < C_in; ++c_in) {\n for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) {\n int t = l - P + k * dilation;\n float x_val = 0.0f;\n if (t >= 0 && t < L) {\n x_val = input[((b * C_in) + c_in) * L + t];\n }\n float w_val = weight[((c_out * C_in) + c_in) * K + k];\n acc += round_tf32(w_val) * round_tf32(x_val);\n }\n }\n \n output[((b * C_out) + c_out) * L + l] = acc;\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor causal_conv1d_cuda(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int kernel_size, int dilation) {\n int B = input.size(0);\n int C_in = input.size(1);\n int L = input.size(2);\n int C_out = weight.size(0);\n \n auto output = torch::empty({B, C_out, L}, input.options());\n \n int total = B * C_out * L;\n const int threads = 256;\n const int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads;\n \n const float* bias_ptr = nullptr;\n if (bias.has_value() && bias.value().defined()) {\n bias_ptr = bias.value().data_ptr<float>();\n }\n \n causal_conv1d_tf32_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias_ptr, output.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, C_in, C_out, L, kernel_size, dilation\n );\n \n return output;\n}\n'''\n\nmodule = load_inline(\n name='causal_conv1d_tf32',\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=['causal_conv1d_cuda'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math', '-arch=sm_90'],\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation=1, bias=False):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.causal_padding = (kernel_size - 1) * dilation\n self.conv1d = nn.Conv1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation=dilation, bias=bias)\n self.kernel_size = kernel_size\n self.dilation = dilation\n\n def forward(self, x):\n return module.causal_conv1d_cuda(x.contiguous(), self.conv1d.weight.contiguous(), self.conv1d.bias, self.kernel_size, self.dilation)\n" }
