Wait, some sources say you can convert to TF32 using bit manipulation. TF32 has the same sign and exponent bits as FP32, but only 10 mantissa bits. So you can quantize to TF32 by zeroing the lower mantissa bits and rounding appropriately. This is equivalent to: ```cpp uint32_t u; memcpy(&u, &x, 4); u = (u + 0x1000) & 0xFFFFE000; // round to nearest even for the 13th bit? ``` Wait, fp32 mantissa is 23 bits. TF32 mantissa is 10 bits. So need to round to nearest even at the 11th bit below the top (i.e., bit 13 counting from LSB?). Actually, bits position: [31] sign, [30:23] exponent, [22:0] mantissa. In tf32, mantissa is [22:13]? No, tf32 format is: - bit 31: sign - bits 30:23: exponent (8 bits) - bits 22:13: mantissa (10 bits) - bits 12:0: zero So to round FP32 to TF32, you can mask the lower 13 bits with proper rounding. Specifically: FP32 value with mantissa `m_22...m_0`. To round to nearest even to 10 bits (i.e., keep `m_22...m_13`), you look at bit 12 (the guard bit) and the remainder. If bit 12 is 0: truncate. If bit 12 is 1 and bits 11:0 are not all zero: round up. If bit 12 is 1 and bits 11:0 are all zero: round to even (i.e., keep bit 13 as is, if it's 1, round up? Actually round to even means if bit 12 is 1 and the truncated part is exactly half, round such that the last kept bit (bit 13) is even). This is basically `float` -> `float` where the lower 13 bits are zeroed with rounding. We can implement this with integer bit-twiddling. Would this match cuDNN's TF32 conversion exactly? cuDNN uses hardware TF32 rounding which, I believe, on Ampere/Hopper devices corresponds to rounding to nearest even at bit 12. But hardware might use an explicit `fma.rn.tf32` that truncates or rounds the operands. However, if we manually round the operands before multiplying and accumulating in FP32, we should get exactly the same result as a software TF32 simulation (but might differ from hardware if hardware also rounds the product). Actually, `mma` with tf32 rounds the operands to tf32 before multiplication, then multiplies exactly (as if the operands are tf32), then accumulates in f32. This is equivalent to rounding the inputs to tf32 before doing fma in f32? Yes! Because tf32 multiplication of two rounded tf32 numbers followed by f32 addition is equivalent to `fma(f32, rounded_a, rounded_b, acc)`, except that hardware MMA does it with exact tf32 multiplication (no rounding in product) and then exact accumulation. Since tf32 has 10-bit mantissa, the product has at most 20-bit mantissa (actually 21 with leading 1), which fits exactly in FP32 (24 bits). So there is no rounding in the product step. Therefore, a sequence of `acc += rounded_a * rounded_b` using FP32 FMA should be numerically identical to using the TF32 tensor core, provided that the rounding to tf32 matches. So the plan: round each weight and each input value to tf32 before the multiply-add. If we do this in shared memory or registers, we match cuDNN TF32 results! Let me implement a helper function: ```cpp __device__ __forceinline__ float to_tf32(float a) { uint32_t u; memcpy(&u, &a, sizeof(uint32_t)); uint32_t bit12 = (u >> 12) & 1; uint32_t lower12 = u & ((1 << 12) - 1); uint32_t mantissa10 = (u >> 13) & ((1 << 10) - 1); if (bit12) { if (lower12 != 0 || (mantissa10 & 1)) { u += (1u << 13); } } u &= ~((1u << 13) - 1); float b; memcpy(&b, &u, sizeof(float)); return b; } ``` Wait, the round-to-nearest-even rule for rounding to 10 mantissa bits (i.e., keeping bits 22..13, zeroing 12..0): - Let `round_bit` = bit 12. - Let `sticky` = OR of bits 11..0. - If `round_bit == 0`: truncate (clear bits 12..0). - If `round_bit == 1` and `sticky == 1`: round up (add 1 to bit 13, which might carry). - If `round_bit == 1` and `sticky == 0`: round to even. The last kept bit is bit 13. If bit 13 is 1, then adding 1 to bit 13 would make it 0 and carry to bit 14, making the overall number even? Actually, "even" means the integer represented by the kept mantissa bits should be even. So if bit 13 is 1 (mantissa10's LSB is 1), then we round up to make it 0 (even? No, 0 is even, but if we add 1 to bit 13, it flips to 0 and carries to bit 14). So we add 1 if bit 13 is 1, else truncate. Wait, standard round-to-nearest-even: if the remainder is exactly half (round_bit=1, sticky=0), then round to the nearest even number, meaning the last retained bit should be 0 after rounding. So: - If mantissa bit 13 (the LSB of the 10-bit mantissa) is 0: truncate (keep it 0). - If mantissa bit 13 is 1: round up (add 1 to bit 13, which makes it 0 and carries). So: ```cpp __device__ __forceinline__ float round_to_tf32(float a) { uint32_t u; memcpy(&u, &a, 4); uint32_t lower13 = u & 0x1FFF; // bits 12..0 uint32_t round_bit = (u >> 12) & 1; uint32_t sticky = lower13 & 0xFFF; // bits 11..0 uint32_t lsb_kept = (u >> 13) & 1; // bit 13 if (round_bit) { if (sticky || lsb_kept) { u += (1u << 13); } } u &= ~0x1FFFu; // clear lower 13 bits float b; memcpy(&b, &u, 4); return b; } ``` This performs round half to even for the lower 13 bits. If cuDNN hardware uses this exact rounding mode (which it should, as standard for IEEE-like rounding to lower precision), then doing `acc += round_tf32(w) * round_tf32(x)` should match! However, there may be a subtlety: hardware TF32 might truncate rather than round? Or round differently? On Ampere, the Tensor Cores convert FP32 inputs to TF32 with rounding to nearest even (CUDA docs say "TensorFloat-32... rounds FP32 mantissa to 10 bits"). I assume standard round-to-nearest-even. Let's implement this helper and test! Also note: If I do the rounding in the kernel after loading weight/input from global memory, I should match. I'll also try on the direct kernel for simplicity. What if I override the weight storage to transpose it, and compute using `bmm` or `matmul` in a way that: - avoids materialized padding by taking slices - uses efficient GEMM through `torch.matmul` Let's think about how to do this with a single efficient GEMM call. For each sequence position `t`, computing output requires inputs from `t-P` to `t` inclusive where `P = (K-1)*dilation`. This is a filter that slides. I could do the following: 1. Reshape `x` from `[B, C_in, L]` to `[B, L, C_in]`. 2. For each `t`, the involved `C_in` channels are `x[:, t - (K-1)*dilation : t+1 : dilation, :]`. 3. Unfold this into a matrix of shape `[B * L, C_in * K]`. But this requires a strided copy (gathers). For `dilation > 1`, this gets complicated. Actually, with `dilation=1`, `kernel_size=4`: `unfolded` has shape `[B, C_in, L, 4]`. Then `output = unfolded @ weight.T` where `weight` is `[C_out, C_in*4]` and `unfolded` is `[B, L, C_in*4]`. For `dilation=1`: `unfolded` can be obtained via `torch.nn.functional.unfold`? But that's for 2D. For 1D, there's no `unfold`. For `dilation=1`, we can use `as_strided` to get the unfolded tensor without copy! Then reshape and use `matmul`. ```python def forward(self, x): B, C_in, L = x.shape K = self.kernel_size P = self.causal_padding # x is [B, C_in, L] # We need to slide a window of size K over the last dim, left-padded by P # With as_strided, we can create a view of [B, C_in, L, K] # First pad x if P > 0: padding = torch.zeros(B, C_in, P, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) x_padded = torch.cat([padding, x], dim=-1) else: x_padded = x # stride for last dim is 1 # We want shape [B, C_in, L, K] # strides of x_padded: [C_in*(L+P), L+P, 1] # strides for view: [C_in*(L+P), L+P, 1, 1] x_unfold = torch.as_strided( x_padded, size=(B, C_in, L, K), stride=(C_in*(L+P), L+P, 1, 1) ).contiguous() # this copies x_unfold = x_unfold.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).reshape(B, L, C_in * K) w = self.conv1d.weight.view(self.conv1d.weight.size(0), -1).t() out = torch.matmul(x_unfold, w) if self.conv1d.bias is not None: out = out + self.conv1d.bias return out.permute(0, 2, 1) ``` Wait, `torch.as_strided(...).contiguous()` creates a memory copy, so it's equivalent to calling `unfold`. The copy cost is `B * C_in * L * K * 4` bytes. For the given sizes: `32 * 256 * 8192 * 4 * 4 = 1,073,741,824` bytes = 1 GB. That's a massive data expansion, which is why the standard approach uses im2col internally but it's optimized. This GEMM-based approach with explicit copy would be very expensive. OK, so the custom direct kernel path is going to be memory bandwidth bound, likely slower than the cuDNN GEMM approach. Actually, looking at the problem again: - B=32, C_in=256, C_out=256, L=8192, K=4, dilation=1 - Total output elements = 32 * 256 * 8192 = 67,108,864 - With 256 threads per block, need ~262,144 blocks Each thread does 256 * 4 = 1024 FMAs. The issue is memory bandwidth. Each thread reads: - 256 input values (one per channel) but scattered across memory - 256 * 4 = 1024 weights (but weights are the same for all threads in the same c_out) This will be very memory bound. PyTorch's cuDNN likely does much better. However, for causal conv with small kernel size, it's possible that a custom kernel could win by avoiding the padding overhead and memory movement. But I'm not confident. Given time constraints, let me submit the correct kernel.

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cpp_source = '''
torch::Tensor causal_conv1d_cuda(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int kernel_size, int dilation);
'''

cuda_source = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <cuda.h>
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <cstdint>

__device__ __forceinline__ float round_tf32(float a) {
    uint32_t u;
    memcpy(&u, &a, sizeof(uint32_t));
    uint32_t lower13 = u & 0x1FFFu;
    uint32_t round_bit = (u >> 12) & 1;
    uint32_t sticky = lower13 & 0xFFFu;
    uint32_t lsb_kept = (u >> 13) & 1;
    if (round_bit) {
        if (sticky || lsb_kept) {
            u += (1u << 13);
        }
    }
    u &= ~0x1FFFu;
    float b;
    memcpy(&b, &u, sizeof(uint32_t));
    return b;
}

__global__ void causal_conv1d_direct_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ input,
    const float* __restrict__ weight,
    const float* __restrict__ bias,
    float* __restrict__ output,
    int B, int C_in, int C_out, int L, int K, int dilation
) {
    int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;
    int total_threads = B * C_out * L;
    if (idx >= total_threads) return;
    
    int l = idx % L;
    int tmp = idx / L;
    int c_out = tmp % C_out;
    int b = tmp / C_out;
    
    const int P = (K - 1) * dilation;
    
    float acc = 0.0f;
    if (bias != nullptr) {
        acc = bias[c_out];
    }
    
    for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) {
        for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) {
            int t = l - P + k * dilation;
            float x_val = 0.0f;
            if (t >= 0 && t < L) {
                x_val = input[((b * C_in) + c_in) * L + t];
            }
            float w_val = weight[((c_out * C_in) + c_in) * K + k];
            acc += round_tf32(w_val) * round_tf32(x_val);
        }
    }
    
    output[((b * C_out) + c_out) * L + l] = acc;
}

torch::Tensor causal_conv1d_cuda(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int kernel_size, int dilation) {
    int B = input.size(0);
    int C_in = input.size(1);
    int L = input.size(2);
    int C_out = weight.size(0);
    
    auto output = torch::empty({B, C_out, L}, input.options());
    
    int total = B * C_out * L;
    const int threads = 256;
    const int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads;
    
    const float* bias_ptr = nullptr;
    if (bias.has_value() && bias.value().defined()) {
        bias_ptr = bias.value().data_ptr<float>();
    }
    
    causal_conv1d_direct_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(
        input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias_ptr, output.data_ptr<float>(),
        B, C_in, C_out, L, kernel_size, dilation
    );
    
    return output;
}
'''

module = load_inline(
    name='causal_conv1d_final',
    cpp_sources=cpp_source,
    cuda_sources=cuda_source,
    functions=['causal_conv1d_cuda'],
    extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'],
)

class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation=1, bias=False):
        super(ModelNew, self).__init__()
        self.causal_padding = (kernel_size - 1) * dilation
        self.conv1d = nn.Conv1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation=dilation, bias=bias)
        self.kernel_size = kernel_size
        self.dilation = dilation

    def forward(self, x):
        return module.causal_conv1d_cuda(x.contiguous(), self.conv1d.weight.contiguous(), self.conv1d.bias, self.kernel_size, self.dilation)
